reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_1x1, reduce_3x3, out_3x3, reduce_5x5, out_5x5, pool_proj):
        """
        :param in_channels: Number of input channels
        :param out_1x1: Number of output channels for the 1x1 convolution
        :param reduce_3x3: Number of output channels for the 1x1 reduction before 3x3 convolution
        :param out_3x3: Number of output channels for the 3x3 convolution
        :param reduce_5x5: Number of output channels for the 1x1 reduction before 5x5 convolution
        :param out_5x5: Number of output channels for the 5x5 convolution
        :param pool_proj: Number of output channels for the pooling projection
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        
        # 1x1 convolution branch
        self.branch1x1 = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_1x1, kernel_size=1)
        
        # 3x3 convolution branch
        self.branch3x3 = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Conv2d(in_channels, reduce_3x3, kernel_size=1),
            nn.Conv2d(reduce_3x3, out_3x3, kernel_size=3, padding=1)
        )
        
        # 5x5 convolution branch
        self.branch5x5 = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Conv2d(in_channels, reduce_5x5, kernel_size=1),
            nn.Conv2d(reduce_5x5, out_5x5, kernel_size=5, padding=2)
        )
        
        # Max pooling branch
        self.branch_pool = nn.Sequential(
            nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=3, stride=1, padding=1),
            nn.Conv2d(in_channels, pool_proj, kernel_size=1)
        )
    
    def forward(self, x):
        """
        :param x: Input tensor, shape (batch_size, in_channels, height, width)
        :return: Output tensor, shape (batch_size, out_channels, height, width)
        """
        branch1x1 = self.branch1x1(x)
        branch3x3 = self.branch3x3(x)
        branch5x5 = self.branch5x5(x)
        branch_pool = self.branch_pool(x)
        
        outputs = [branch1x1, branch3x3, branch5x5, branch_pool]
        return torch.cat(outputs, 1)

# Test code
in_channels = 480
out_1x1 = 192
reduce_3x3 = 96
out_3x3 = 208
reduce_5x5 = 16
out_5x5 = 48
pool_proj = 64
batch_size = 10
height = 224
width = 224

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_1x1, reduce_3x3, out_3x3, reduce_5x5, out_5x5, pool_proj]

How to compute it efficiently? We could use im2col + GEMM. im2col for 3x3 with padding 1 on input of shape (B, C_in, H, W) yields a matrix of shape (B*H*W, C_in*9). Then GEMM: (B*H*W, C_in*9) @ (C_in*9, out_3x3) -> (B*H*W, out_3x3). This uses cuBLAS, which is fast. Memory: im2col produces a large intermediate: B*H*W * C_in * 9 floats. For B=10, H=W=224, C_in=480: 10*50176*480*9 = 2,167,603,200 floats = 8.67 GB. That's huge. It might not fit in memory? 8.67 GB is large but might be okay on a high-end GPU with 24GB or 40GB. However, it would be slow due to memory allocation and bandwidth. PyTorch's cuDNN uses implicit GEMM (without explicit im2col) which is more memory efficient. For branch5x5 (fused): im2col would be B*H*W * C_in * 25 = 24 GB. That's too big. So im2col is not viable. Thus we need a more clever direct convolution kernel. Alternative: use CUTLASS? We can't easily import CUTLASS in a load_inline extension without building it, which is complex. Alternative: use `cudnn` via PyTorch's C++ API? We can call `at::conv2d` in C++. That would use cuDNN. Then we just need to fuse the concatenation. But can we call `at::conv2d` and write directly into a slice? In PyTorch C++ API, `at::conv2d` returns a new tensor. We cannot pass an output tensor. So we still need to copy into the final output. However, we could allocate the output tensor and then use `at::conv2d` for each branch into a temporary tensor, and then copy each temporary into the output slice using a custom kernel or `tensor.copy_`. The copy is a memory bandwidth bound operation but the temporary tensors are already computed by cuDNN. The speedup might be small because the reference already does this but with `torch.cat` which also copies. Actually `torch.cat` is quite efficient, but it does read all inputs and write output. So the fused version would save the cat copy but still allocate temporaries and copy. That might be a minor speedup. But the reference also has to allocate temporaries. The main issue is that the reference does separate convs (which allocate their own outputs) and then cat (which copies them). In our version, we can do separate convs and then a custom kernel that copies them into the output. But that's basically what cat does. So speedup would be negligible. Wait: but the reference uses `nn.Conv2d`, which computes each branch sequentially. Could we run them in parallel? CUDA kernels from different streams? `load_inline` functions run on the default stream unless we use streams. We could use multiple CUDA streams to overlap computation of different branches. Since the branches are independent, we can run them on different streams. That might yield a speedup because the GPU is underutilized by a single conv? Actually cuDNN convs are usually very GPU-utilized. Running them in parallel might not help unless the GPU has spare resources. But with large convs, they already saturate the GPU. However, the smaller branches (5x5 and pool) might run concurrently with larger ones if we use streams. But PyTorch's default stream is the same for all ops, so they run sequentially. If we launch each branch in a separate stream, the GPU scheduler might interleave them. But with large FLOPs, memory bandwidth might be saturated, so parallel streams may not help. But there is a trick: use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` to write a CUDA kernel that calls cuDNN functions? Actually, we can call `at::conv2d` inside C++ code. But we cannot easily use it in a CUDA kernel (device code). We can write a host function that calls `at::conv2d` multiple times and then concatenates. That is just a C++ extension, not a custom CUDA kernel. The problem explicitly says "custom CUDA kernel". Usually the evaluation expects a `__global__` function. Let's revisit the task. It's a typical "kernel optimization" problem from some benchmark (maybe KernelBench or TritonBench). The expected approach is often to write a naive fused kernel, and because the reference is memory-bound and does many separate ops, the fused kernel wins despite being naive, due to reduced memory traffic. For example, we can write a kernel that computes everything directly: each thread computes one output pixel for a subset of output channels. Let's analyze the compute intensity per thread if each thread computes all branches for one pixel. For a given output pixel (b,y,x), which is fixed across all output channels, we need: - Branch1x1: dot product over 480 input channels. - Branch3x3: for each of 208 output channels, dot product over 3x3 spatial and 480 input channels? Wait, if we fused the weight, it's a single 3x3 conv with 480 input channels. So for each output channel, dot product over 480*9 = 4320 values. - Branch5x5: similarly, 480*25 = 12000 values per output channel. - Pool branch: max over 3x3 per input channel, then dot product over 480 values per output channel. If we have each thread handle one output channel for one pixel, that would be: - Thread count = B*H*W*total_C = 10*224*224*512 = ~257M threads. That's too many for a single launch? Actually the GPU can handle billions of threads across SMs. 257M threads is fine. But the work per thread varies: - For branch1x1 threads (first 192 channels): dot product over 480 values. -> ~192*480=92k multiplies per pixel. - For branch3x3 threads (next 208 channels): dot product over 4320 values per output channel. -> 208*4320 = ~899k multiplies per pixel. - For branch5x5 threads (next 48 channels): dot product over 12000 values. -> 48*12000 = 576k multiplies. - For pool branch threads (next 64 channels): max over 9 values per input channel + dot over 480 values. -> 64*480 = 30.7k multiplies per pixel. Total multiplies per pixel across all branches = ~1.6M. That's high compute intensity. If each thread does this, it's likely compute-bound. But wait, if each thread is independent, there is no data reuse: each thread will load the same input pixel's neighborhood many times. That would be terrible for memory bandwidth because 257M threads each reading 480 values means reading 257M*480*4 bytes = ~494 GB of input data! But the input is only 1 GB. So there is huge duplication. But if the input fits in L2 cache, maybe it's okay? Actually the L2 cache is only a few MB. The input is 1 GB. So most reads will go to HBM. This is terrible. Thus we need to reuse input data across threads. Tiling approach: process a tile of spatial positions (e.g., a 16x16 block) and a tile of output channels (e.g., 64 channels). Load the required input tile (plus halo) into shared memory. Then each thread computes a subset of output channels for a pixel in the tile. This way the input is loaded once per block and reused by all threads. Let's design such a kernel. Parameters: - Tile size: maybe 16x16 spatial, and we process one output branch at a time? Or all branches together? - Output channels total = 512. We can process output channels in groups of, say, 64 or 128. - Input channels = 480. We can process input channels in groups to fit in shared memory. Shared memory capacity: assume 164 KB per SM (or 96 KB). We want to use at most 48 KB to allow high occupancy. If we process a spatial tile of 16x16, and input channels in chunks of C_in_block. For the 5x5 branch, we need a halo of 2 on each side, so input tile size = 20x20. For 3x3, halo of 1, so 18x18. For 1x1 and pool, no halo, 16x16. If we load the full input tile for 5x5: 20*20*C_in_block floats. If C_in_block=16, that's 6400 floats = 25.6 KB. If C_in_block=32, that's 12.8KB? Wait, 20*20=400, *32 = 12800 floats = 51.2 KB. That's a bit high. C_in_block=16 gives 25.6KB. We also need weight storage in shared memory? That might be too much. For each output channel tile, we iterate over input channel blocks. For each input channel block, we load the spatial tile for those channels into shared memory. Then we compute partial sums for the output channels. We also need weight storage. If we have a tile of 64 output channels, and input block of 16 channels, and kernel size up to 5x5, weight size = 64 * 16 * 25 = 25,600 floats = 102.4 KB. That's too big for shared memory. Alternative: load weight tile into shared memory but smaller, or keep weights in registers or global memory with caching. The weights are only ~5.6 MB total (fused). They might fit in L2 cache. For a given output channel, the weights are contiguous across input channels. In global memory, we can load them coalesced. Better approach: don't tile over output channels for the large convs. Instead, tile over spatial dimensions only, and each thread computes a single output channel for its pixel. Use shared memory only for the input tile. The weights are read from global memory. Because weights are reused across the spatial tile, they will be cached in L2. Since the weight footprint for a branch is small (e.g., fused 3x3 weights: 3.4 MB; fused 5x5: 2.2 MB), they might stay in L2. So design: - For each branch (or all branches together), we launch a grid where each block handles a tile of e.g. 16x16 spatial positions. - Each block loads the required input tile (with halo) for a chunk of input channels (or all input channels?). - If we load all input channels for the spatial tile, shared memory is too big. So we must iterate over input channel chunks. - For each input channel chunk of size, say, 8 or 16, load spatial tile + halo for those channels. - For each thread (corresponding to a specific output channel and spatial position), accumulate partial dot products. But each thread would need to know which branch and which output channel it belongs to. We can map threads to output channels and spatial positions. Let's decide on thread mapping. Total output = (B, out_C, H, W). out_C = 512. We can map block to (blockIdx.x over B * H_tiles * W_tiles), blockIdx.y over output channel tiles. Block dimension: say 16x16 threads for spatial, but we also need to cover output channels. Let me analyze the reference model. It's an Inception module with four branches: 1. 1×1 conv (in_channels → out_1x1) 2. 1×1 conv (in_channels → reduce_3x3), then 3×3 conv (reduce_3x3 → out_3x3, padding=1) 3. 1×1 conv (in_channels → reduce_5x5), then 5×5 conv (reduce_5x5 → out_5x5, padding=2) 4. Overhead from multiple kernel launches, repeated reading of input (4 times! because each branch reads input independently in PyTorch). Fusing all this into one kernel can give huge speedup by reading input once and computing all branches. How to fuse? A single CUDA kernel that loads input tiles, loads weights into shared memory, and computes all four branches. However, with the large input channels (480) and large output channels (512 total), doing all in one kernel is complex. But we can at least fuse per-branch fully and maybe do better. Simple approach: Use CUTLASS/cuDNN? No, we need a custom kernel. Let me think about the best approach. Since we're on H100 with 3.35 TB/s bandwidth, the main inefficiency of the reference is: 1. Input is read 4 times (once per branch) 2. Output writes are separated (4 writes instead of 1) 3. Intermediate activations for 3x3 and 5x5 branches are written and read. If we fuse everything into one kernel: - Read input once - Compute all branches on the fly - Write concatenated output once This could save a lot of memory bandwidth. The total MACs is proportional to the number of spatial positions times output channels times C_TILE. Threads can be assigned linearly over the (spatial, out_channel) domain. Let's define `N1 = TH*TW*o1`, `N3 = (TH+2)*(TW+2)*r3`, `N5 = (TH+4)*(TW+4)*r5`, `Np = TH*TW*op`. Total accumulators to update = N1+N3+N5+Np. With blockDim.x = 256, each thread handles `total / 256` accumulators. But for branch1 and pool_proj, the accumulation is just `val += x[c] * W[o][c]`. For z3 and z5, it's also `val += x[c] * W[r][c]`. So it's all the same pattern: for each assigned accumulator (pixel, out_ch), loop over c in C_TILE and accumulate. Maxpool complication: for pool_proj, the accumulator is `yp[pixel][op] += max_{dh,dw}(x[pixel_offset][c]) * Wp[op][c]`. The max is over 3×3 spatial positions. This means we can't just do a simple dot product; we need x values from neighbors. But the max is per channel c. So for each c, we need to find max_x = max(x[nbhd][c]), then `yp += max_x * Wp[op][c]`. If we tile over C_TILE, for each c in the tile, for each spatial pixel in the output tile, we need to find the max of x in the 3×3 neighborhood, then update yp accumulators. This breaks the simple parallel assignment because for a given c, all op accumulators for a pixel depend on the same max_x. But we can still parallelize over (pixel, op). For each thread assigned to (pixel, op), it needs max_x for that pixel. We could precompute max_x for all pixels and store in a small buffer (TH*TW floats per c), or compute it on the fly. Since C_TILE is small (say 32), and TH*TW is small (say 16), max_x buffer is 16 floats. We can compute it per thread or collaboratively. Actually, for pool_proj: For each output pixel (h,w), p(h,w,c) = max_{dh,dw} x[h+dh][w+dw][c]. Then yp(h,w,op) += sum_c p(h,w,c) * Wp[op][c]. We can iterate c in C_TILE. For each c, all threads collaboratively compute p(h,w,c) for all (h,w) and store in shared `pool_smem[TH][TW]`. Then each thread updates its yp accumulators: `yp_acc[op] += pool_smem[h][w] * Wp[op][c]`. pool_smem: 4×4 = 16 floats = 64 bytes. Tiny! So algorithm outline for block: 1. Init accumulators in registers/shared: - y1_acc for assigned (h,w,o1): size N1 / num_threads. - z3_acc for assigned (h,w,r3): size N3 / num_threads. Actually z3_acc in shared memory. - z5_acc for assigned (h,w,r5): size N5 / num_threads. In shared. - yp_acc for assigned (h,w,op): size Np / num_threads. In registers. 2. For C in chunks of C_TILE: a. Load x_tile[ ][ ][C_TILE] into x_smem. b. Load W1_tile, W3_1_tile, W5_1_tile, Wp_tile into weight smem (or directly from global). c. __syncthreads(). d. Compute: - For each thread's assigned branch1 accumulators: load x and W1, accumulate. - For each thread's assigned z3 accumulators: load x and W3_1, accumulate into z3_smem. - For each thread's assigned z5 accumulators: load x and W5_1, accumulate into z5_smem. - For pool: first, all threads compute p_smem[h][w] = max over 3x3 of x_smem for current c. Then each thread updates yp_acc. e. __syncthreads(). 3. After C loop: - z3_smem and z5_smem now hold full intermediates. - y1_acc registers hold full branch1 outputs. - yp_acc registers hold full pool_proj outputs. 4. No, write y3 directly to global out. - __syncthreads(). 5. Compute branch5_2 similarly. 6. Write y1, y3, y5, yp to global output. This is complex but manageable. The key is assigning work to threads. Total units: TH*TW*op = 16*64 = 1024. Each thread handles 8 units. We can keep as is, or flatten to (o3, r3*9). Then if we process by o3, load is strided by r3*9. - w5_2: (o5, r5, 5, 5). With transposed 1×1 weights: For branch1: `w1_t[c*o1 + o]`. This is excellent. Similarly for z3: w3_1_t[c*r3 + r]. Threads with consecutive r will be coalesced. Can we also transpose w3_2 and w5_2? w3_2 is (o3, r3, 3, 3). We can view as (o3, r3*9). If we transpose to (r3*9, o3), then threads with consecutive o access consecutive memory. But then loop over r is outer, o inner. We can restructure: For each r and each spatial offset (9 values), load w3_2_t[(r*9+offset)*o3 + o]. If threads in warp have consecutive o, these are consecutive. Coalesced! So we should transpose w3_2 to (r3*9, o3). Similarly w5_2 to (r5*25, o5). This weight transposition will dramatically improve memory coalescing in the spatial conv phases too. So on host side: ```python self.w1_t = self.branch1x1.weight.view(o1, C).t().contiguous() self.b1 = self.branch1x1.bias.contiguous() self.w3_1_t = self.branch3x3[0].weight.view(r3, C).t().contiguous() self.b3_1 = self.branch3x3[0].bias.contiguous() self.w3_2_t = self.branch3x3[1].weight.view(o3, r3, 3, 3).permute(1,2,3,0).contiguous().view(r3*9, o3) self.b3_2 = self.branch3x3[1].bias.contiguous() # etc. ``` Wait, `permute(1,2,3,0)` gives (r3, 3, 3, o3). Then `.view(r3*9, o3)`. Yes. Similarly for w5_2: `w5_2_t = branch5x5[1].weight.view(o5, r5, 5, 5).permute(1,2,3,0).contiguous().view(r5*25, o5)`. And for w1, w3_1, w5_1, wp: just transpose the first two dims (after flattening kernel). Also biases are (Out,). This is a one-time setup cost in __init__. Very cheap. With transposed weights, the kernel's global memory access pattern becomes much better. Let's also consider using `float4` loads for weights when possible. If o1=192, and warp accesses 32 consecutive o, we can load float4 (4 floats) per thread, giving 128 bytes per instruction, fully utilizing the bus. We need the output channel dimensions to be multiples of 4 for float4 to be clean. 192, 96, 208, 16, 48, 64. 208 % 4 = 0. 16,48,64,96,192 are multiples of 4. So float4 is usable for most. For 208, still multiple of 4. Great! We can use float4 in the dot product loop: each thread processes 4 channels at a time. But then the reduction sum needs to be scalar. Actually, using float4 for weights is great for memory bandwidth. For compute, we still need scalar FMAs. We can do: ```cpp float4 w4 = *(float4*)(w_ptr + base + c); acc0 += x_c * w4.x; acc1 += x_c * w4.y; acc2 += x_c * w4.z; acc3 += x_c * w4.w; ``` This requires keeping 4 accumulators. Fine. For x_c, we load it from shared memory. We can also try to load x in chunks, but x is broadcast. Actually, maybe we can load x into registers for a group of channels. E.g., load x[c..c+3] as float4. Then for each o group, do dot product. But x varies per channel, w varies per o. `sum_c x[c] * w[c, o]`. To use vectorized x, we'd need w stored as (o, c) with c vectorized, which is not how we transposed it. With w transposed to (C, o), for fixed c, w[c, o0..o3] is contiguous. So float4 load of w is natural. Let's write the inner loop with float4: For a thread handling 24 output channels (e.g., o=o_start..o_start+23): We can process o in steps of 4. But `o_start` might not be aligned to 4. We can ensure alignment by assigning groups that are multiples of 4. With TPC=8 and o1=192, 192/8=24. 24 is multiple of 4. Good. Let's define: each thread in the pixel group handles a contiguous chunk of output channels, and the chunk size is a multiple of 4. But for the linear assignment over all threads (which is simpler), we can just make the loop over o use float4 where possible. Actually, let's keep it simple first. Use scalar floats and rely on coalescing + L2 cache. Let me write the full kernel code and host wrapper. I'll write `fused_inception_forward` in C++. Actually, just to make the kernel simpler, I'll avoid `TH`/`TW` template macros and just use defines. Since the test case is fixed, but we want generality. We can make TH and TW arguments or fixed. Let me make them fixed: #define TH 4, #define TW 4. The kernel will work for any H,W, but tile size is fixed. This is fine as long as H and W are multiples of 1 (they are, 224). The kernel generically handles boundaries. Shared memory usage with TH=4, TW=4: 140.8KB. That's okay. Block size 256. Let me draft the code. One issue: the number of input channels `C` is used in smem size calculation and indexing. Since C is an argument, dynamic smem size is computed at runtime. In the kernel, I can use 1D indexing for everything. Let me write the C++ kernel: ```cpp #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cuda_fp16.h> #define TH 4 #define TW 4 __global__ void inception_kernel( const float* x, const float* w1_t, const float* b1, const float* w3_1_t, const float* b3_1, const float* w3_2_t, const float* b3_2, const float* w5_1_t, const float* b5_1, const float* w5_2_t, const float* b5_2, const float* wp_t, const float* bp, float* out, int B, int C, int H, int W, int o1, int r3, int o3, int r5, int o5, int op) { int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; int b = blockIdx.z; int tile_h = blockIdx.y * TH; int tile_w = blockIdx.x * TW; int x_area = (TH + 4) * (TW + 4); int z3_area = (TH + 2) * (TW + 2); int z5_area = (TH + 4) * (TW + 4); extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* x_smem = smem; float* z3_smem = x_smem + x_area * C; float* z5_smem = z3_smem + z3_area * r3; // Load x_smem with zero padding int x_smem_size = x_area * C; for (int idx = tid; idx < x_smem_size; idx += num_threads) { int c = idx % C; int rem = idx / C; int iwi = rem % (TW + 4); int ihi = rem / (TW + 4); int hi = tile_h + ihi - 2; int wi = tile_w + iwi - 2; float val = 0.0f; if (hi >= 0 && hi < H && wi >= 0 && wi < W) { val = x[((b * H + hi) * W + wi) * C + c]; } x_smem[idx] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute z3_smem int z3_smem_size = z3_area * r3; for (int idx = tid; idx < z3_smem_size; idx += num_threads) { int r = idx % r3; int rem = idx / r3; int iwi = rem % (TW + 2); int ihi = rem / (TW + 2); int x_hi = ihi + 1; int x_wi = iwi + 1; int x_offset = x_hi * (TW + 4) + x_wi; float acc = b3_1[r]; for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { acc += x_smem[x_offset * C + c] * w3_1_t[c * r3 + r]; } z3_smem[idx] = acc; } __syncthreads(); // Compute z5_smem int z5_smem_size = z5_area * r5; for (int idx = tid; idx < z5_smem_size; idx += num_threads) { int r = idx % r5; int rem = idx / r5; int iwi = rem % (TW + 4); int ihi = rem / (TW + 4); int x_offset = ihi * (TW + 4) + iwi; float acc = b5_1[r]; for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { acc += x_smem[x_offset * C + c] * w5_1_t[c * r5 + r]; } z5_smem[idx] = acc; } __syncthreads(); int total_out = o1 + o3 + o5 + op; // Branch1 int branch1_size = TH * TW * o1; for (int idx = tid; idx < branch1_size; idx += num_threads) { int o = idx % o1; int rem = idx / o1; int pw = rem % TW; int ph = rem / TW; int h = tile_h + ph; int w = tile_w + pw; if (h < H && w < W) { int x_offset = (ph + 2) * (TW + 4) + (pw + 2); float acc = b1[o]; for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { acc += x_smem[x_offset * C + c] * w1_t[c * o1 + o]; } out[((b * total_out + o) * H + h) * W + w] = acc; } } // Pool projection int pool_size = TH * TW * op; for (int idx = tid; idx < pool_size; idx += num_threads) { int o = idx % op; int rem = idx / op; int pw = rem % TW; int ph = rem / TW; int h = tile_h + ph; int w = tile_w + pw; if (h < H && w < W) { int x_base = (ph + 2) * (TW + 4) + (pw + 2); float acc = bp[o]; for (int c = 0; c < C; ++c) { float max_val = x_smem[x_base * C + c]; #pragma unroll for (int dh = -1; dh <= 1; ++dh) { for (int dw = -1; dw <= 1; ++dw) { int nb_offset = (ph + 2 + dh) * (TW + 4) + (pw + 2 + dw); float v = x_smem[nb_offset * C + c]; if (v > max_val) max_val = v; } } acc += max_val * wp_t[c * op + o]; } out[((b * total_out + (o1 + o3 + o5 + o)) * H + h) * W + w] = acc; } } // Branch3_2 int branch3_size = TH * TW * o3; for (int idx = tid; idx < branch3_size; idx += num_threads) { int o = idx % o3; int rem = idx / o3; int pw = rem % TW; int ph = rem / TW; int h = tile_h + ph; int w = tile_w + pw; if (h < H && w < W) { int z3_base = (ph + 1) * (TW + 2) + (pw + 1); float acc = b3_2[o]; for (int r = 0; r < r3; ++r) { #pragma unroll for (int dh = -1; dh <= 1; ++dh) { for (int dw = -1; dw <= 1; ++dw) { int z3_offset = (ph + 1 + dh) * (TW + 2) + (pw + 1 + dw); float z = z3_smem[z3_offset * r3 + r]; float w_val = w3_2_t[((r * 3 + (dh + 1)) * 3 + (dw + 1)) * o3 + o]; acc += z * w_val; } } } out[((b * total_out + (o1 + o)) * H + h) * W + w] = acc; } } // Branch5_2 int branch5_size = TH * TW * o5; for (int idx = tid; idx < branch5_size; idx += num_threads) { int o = idx % o5; int rem = idx / o5; int pw = rem % TW; int ph = rem / TW; int h = tile_h + ph; int w = tile_w + pw; if (h < H && w < W) { int z5_base = (ph + 2) * (TW + 4) + (pw + 2); float acc = b5_2[o]; for (int r = 0; r < r5; ++r) { #pragma unroll for (int dh = -2; dh <= 2; ++dh) { for (int dw = -2; dw <= 2; ++dw) { int z5_offset = (ph + 2 + dh) * (TW + 4) + (pw + 2 + dw); float z = z5_smem[z5_offset * r5 + r]; float w_val = w5_2_t[((r * 5 + (dh + 2)) * 5 + (dw + 2)) * o5 + o]; acc += z * w_val; } } } out[((b * total_out + (o1 + o3 + o)) * H + h) * W + w] = acc; } } } ``` Wait, the w3_2_t indexing: `w3_2_t` has shape (r3*9, o3). So index is `(flat_spatial * o3 + o)`. `flat_spatial = r*9 + (dh+1)*3 + (dw+1)`. So `((r * 3 + (dh+1)) * 3 + (dw+1)) * o3 + o`. Yes. Similarly for w5_2_t: `((r * 5 + (dh+2)) * 5 + (dw+2)) * o5 + o`. The transposed weights for 1×1 are (C, o1), so index `c * o1 + o`. Yes. Biases are accessed via `b1[o]` etc. Biases are small and cached. One concern: `__syncthreads()` between phases. In phase 1, we compute z3 and z5. Then phase 2 reads them. Need sync. Between z3 and z5, do we need sync? No, because they write to disjoint smem regions. But both read x_smem, which is already written. After the `__syncthreads()` post x_smem load, we can compute z3 and z5 concurrently (no sync needed between them). Then a final `__syncthreads()` before phase 2. However, since they're in the same loop (`for idx = tid; ...`), all threads participate in both. z3 and z5 are computed by all threads in separate loops. No need for sync between z3 and z5, but we do need a sync after both are computed. Also, `x_smem` is loaded first. But what if some threads finish z3 before others start? They don't interfere. So one __syncthreads after x load, then compute z3 and z5, then __syncthreads, then compute outputs. Another concern: the kernel writes to `out` from multiple phases without synchronization. But each phase writes to disjoint output channel slices. And phases are executed sequentially by the same thread, so no conflict. However, different threads write to different spatial pixels. Within a phase, threads write independently. No race. Wait, `out` is global memory. Are there any threads writing to the same location? Each (b,h,w,o) is unique. So no races. Let's check the pool branch: MaxPool2d with padding=1. For PyTorch, if `kernel_size=3, stride=1, padding=1`, the output size equals input size. Our `if (h < H && w < W)` guard ensures we don't write out of bounds. The maxpool reads x_smem at offsets (ph+2+dh, pw+2+dw) which are within 0..7 for TH=4,TW=4. x_smem has size 8×8, so indices are valid. Good. But for `h=0, w=0` (top-left pixel), maxpool should consider padded zeros at h=-1,w=-1 etc. Our x_smem loaded these as 0 because hi<0. Good. Conv padding: branch3_2 uses z3_smem at (ph+1+dh), etc. z3_smem represents the full 6×6 region for tile_h-1 to tile_h+4. At boundaries, if tile_h=0, ph=0, then ph+1-1=0, which is z3 at tile_h-1 (h=-1). z3 at h=-1 was computed from x at h=-2..h=0? Wait, z3 at h=-1 is computed from x at h=-1 (since branch3_1 is 1×1 conv). x at h=-1 was loaded as 0. So z3 at h=-1 is just b3_1. Then branch3_2 at h=0 uses z3 at h=-1,h=0,h=1. Since padding=1 for branch3_2, that's correct. For branch5_2: z5_smem at tile_h-2 to tile_h+5. At tile_h=0, z5 at indices 0,1 correspond to h=-2,-1. These were computed from x_padded=0. Then branch5_2 at h=0 uses z5 at h=-2,-1,0,1,2. Padding=2, correct. So the kernel is correct. Let's estimate correctness. It should be exact FP32, matching PyTorch exactly almost (order of operations differs, which could cause tiny differences). PyTorch's conv uses im2col + GEMM in a different order, and with bias addition. Our kernel does reduction in the same order (c loop from 0 to C-1). Weight values are the same. So the result should match within 1e-4, likely exactly or very close. One more thing: are we missing the ReLU or activation? Looking at the reference model, there are NO activations between convs! nn.Conv2d without activation. So our direct computation is correct. Wait, `nn.Conv2d` default uses bias=True. We included bias. Good. Now the host-side wrapper and Python setup. ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> // ... CUDA kernel code ... torch::Tensor inception_forward( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w1_t, torch::Tensor b1, ... all weights ... ) { int B = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int H = x.size(2); int W = x.size(3); int o1 = b1.size(0); int r3 = b3_1.size(0); int o3 = b3_2.size(0); int r5 = b5_1.size(0); int o5 = b5_2.size(0); int op = bp.size(0); int total_out = o1 + o3 + o5 + op; auto out = torch::empty({B, total_out, H, W}, x.options()); dim3 block(256); dim3 grid((W + TW - 1) / TW, (H + TH - 1) / TH, B); size_t smem_size = ((TH+4)*(TW+4)*C + (TH+2)*(TW+2)*r3 + (TH+4)*(TW+4)*r5) * sizeof(float); inception_kernel<<<grid, block, smem_size>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), w1_t.data_ptr<float>(), b1.data_ptr<float>(), ... out.data_ptr<float>(), B, C, H, W, o1, r3, o3, r5, o5, op ); return out; } """ functions = ["inception_forward"] ``` We need to make sure the CUDA source is compiled with the C++ extension. Let's do exactly that: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, ...): super().__init__() self.branch1x1 = nn.Conv2d(...) self.branch3x3 = nn.Sequential(...) self.branch5x5 = nn.Sequential(...) self.branch_pool = nn.Sequential(...) def forward(self, x): # Extract weights and biases from the modules w1 = self.branch1x1.weight # (o1, C, 1, 1) b1 = self.branch1x1.bias # (o1,) w3_1 = self.branch3x3[0].weight # (r3, C, 1, 1) ... # Call the custom kernel return inception_forward_cuda(x, w1, b1, ...) ``` In `forward`, we can transpose the weights on the fly: ```python w1_t = w1.view(o1, C).t().contiguous() ``` This involves a memory copy of 92k floats = 360KB. Very cheap. We can also do it once and cache, but on the fly is fine. But wait: the custom kernel expects the weights to be transposed. We can transpose them in Python and pass to the C++ function. The C++ function will accept `torch::Tensor`. PyTorch will handle the contiguous conversion if we specify `.contiguous()`. Actually, to reduce overhead, we can compute the transposed weights in `forward()` using `.view().t().contiguous()`. For the tiny weight sizes, this overhead is negligible compared to the forward pass time. Let's verify weight shapes for PyTorch Conv2d: - weight: (out_channels, in_channels, kH, kW) - bias: (out_channels,) So: `w1` shape: (o1, C, 1, 1) `w1_t = w1.view(o1, C).t()` shape: (C, o1) `w3_1` shape: (r3, C, 1, 1) `w3_1_t = w3_1.view(r3, C).t()` shape: (C, r3) `w3_2` shape: (o3, r3, 3, 3) `w3_2_t = w3_2.permute(1, 2, 3, 0).contiguous().view(r3*9, o3)` `w5_1` shape: (r5, C, 1, 1) `w5_1_t = w5_1.view(r5, C).t()` shape: (C, r5) `w5_2` shape: (o5, r5, 5, 5) `w5_2_t = w5_2.permute(1, 2, 3, 0).contiguous().view(r5*25, o5)` `wp` shape: (op, C, 1, 1) `wp_t = wp.view(op, C).t()` shape: (C, op) Transpose overhead: total weight size is 375k floats = 1.5MB. Transpose copies 1.5MB. Negligible. Now, I need to write the complete Python code. Let's do it. I'll add `-O3` and maybe `--use_fast_math` but we need fp32 precision. `--use_fast_math` might use approximate math. Since we don't use `sin`/`exp`, only FMA, it won't affect much. But better safe: use `-O3` without `--use_fast_math`. Wait, does `-O3` include vectorization? It might. Let's add `extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "-lineinfo"]` or just `"-O3"`. One more optimization: use `__ldg` (load global through read-only cache) for weights? H100 has global memory loads that go through L2 anyway. But adding `const __restrict__` should enable `__ldg` automatically via compiler. Another optimization: for maxpool, instead of 9 loads and 8 comparisons per channel, we can precompute maxpool for all channels and store in a small `pool_smem[TH][TW]` per channel. But that's what the loop does. Alternatively, compute maxpool for all channels collaboratively. But it's fine. Let's also try to vectorize the bias pointers. No need. I should check if there are any issues with `TH` and `TW` not dividing `H` and `W`. The loop `for (idx=tid; idx<branch1_size; idx+=num_threads)` where `branch1_size = TH*TW*o1` doesn't depend on H,W. But `h = tile_h + ph`, and we check `h < H`. If some pixels in the tile are out of bounds, threads skip them (no write). However, in x_smem loading, we load the full padded tile, with OOB set to 0. For `tile_h = H - H%TH` (since grid is `(H+TH-1)/TH`), the last tile might have some pixels where h >= H. The writes are skipped. But the z3_smem, z5_smem are still computed for the full tile, including regions where h>=H. This is fine because they won't be used for output? Wait, branch3_2 for a pixel (ph,pw) where h>=H uses z3_smem values. But we skip writing. We might also skip computing to save work, but since it's bounded and only the last tile, it's okay. Actually, for `h >= H`, we still compute the value and then drop it. That's some wasted work on the last row/col tiles but negligible. Let me think about whether any z3 or z5 values computed for OOB regions are needed by valid pixels. For valid pixel at h=H-1, w=W-1, if it's in a tile that extends beyond H/W, its 3×3 or 5×5 neighborhood might need pixels from OOB areas. Those OOB pixels in the NEXT tile? No, neighborhoods are fully within the tile because the tile loads the halo. So z3 for the tile's local pixels handles it. The extra pixels beyond H/W aren't needed by valid outputs in this tile. But we compute them anyway. Fine. Let's code it up and compile. I need to make sure `out` tensor is contiguous and on the same device as `x`. reasoning
The issue is that `dim3` and the kernel launch syntax `<<< >>>` are CUDA-specific and must go inside the `.cu` file, not the C++ `.cpp` file. `load_inline` puts `cpp_sources` into `main.cpp` and `cuda_sources` into `cuda.cu`. I need to put the wrapper function inside the CUDA source file instead of the cpp source file, so it can use CUDA syntax. Actually, let me check: the wrapper function `inception_forward` needs to be exposed. It can be in the cuda_sources file, and then referenced in cpp_sources with a declaration, or I can just put the entire wrapper in the cuda_sources file and have it be the only source for the function. Let me restructure: put the CUDA kernel and the wrapper function all in the `cuda_sources`, and in `cpp_sources` just put a declaration `torch::Tensor inception_forward(...);` or just put the wrapper in cuda_sources and no cpp_sources. Actually, I think if I just put everything in `cuda_sources` and keep `cpp_sources` empty or with just declarations, it should work. Let me try that. Also I noticed warnings about unused variables z3_base and z5_base, I'll remove them. Let me restructure the code properly.

reasoning
The issue is that in the C++ function inside the .cu file, the constant names `TH` and `TW` are being replaced by 4, so it says `const int 4 = 4;`. The define macros at the top of the file get replaced everywhere. Since in the host function I say: ``` const int TH = 4; ``` This becomes: ``` const int 4 = 4; ``` I need to avoid reusing `TH` and `TW` in the host code, or use other names that don't conflict. Let me fix this.
